            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2009 41:12"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Jun Wang and colleagues report the genome sequence of the cucumber. The cucumber genome is the seventh plant genome sequence to be reported and was assembled with a combination of traditional Sanger and next-generation sequencing methods. Cucumber is an economically important crop as well as a model system for sex determination studies and plant vascular biology. Here we report the draft genome sequence of Cucumis sativus var. sativus L., assembled using a novel combination of traditional Sanger and next-generation Illumina GA sequencing technologies to obtain 72.2-fold genome coverage. The absence of recent whole-genome duplication, along with the presence of few tandem duplications, explains the small number of genes in the cucumber. Our study establishes that five of the cucumber&#39;s seven chromosomes arose from fusions of ten ancestral chromosomes after divergence from Cucumis melo. The sequenced cucumber genome affords insight into traits such as its sex expression, disease resistance, biosynthesis of cucurbitacin and &#39;fresh green&#39; odor. We also identify 686 gene clusters related to phloem function. The cucumber genome provides a valuable resource for developing elite cultivars and for studying the evolution and function of the plant vascular system."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Cucumber is an economically important crop as well as a model system for sex determination studies and plant vascular biology. Here we report the draft genome sequence of <i>Cucumis sativus</i> var. <i>sativus</i> L., assembled using a novel combination of traditional Sanger and next-generation Illumina GA sequencing technologies to obtain 72.2-fold genome coverage. The absence of recent whole-genome duplication, along with the presence of few tandem duplications, explains the small number of genes in the cucumber. Our study establishes that five of the cucumber's seven chromosomes arose from fusions of ten ancestral chromosomes after divergence from <i>Cucumis melo</i>. The sequenced cucumber genome affords insight into traits such as its sex expression, disease resistance, biosynthesis of cucurbitacin and 'fresh green' odor. We also identify 686 gene clusters related to phloem function. The cucumber genome provides a valuable resource for developing elite cultivars and for studying the evolution and function of the plant vascular system.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The botanical family Cucurbitaceae, commonly known as cucurbits and gourds, includes several economically important cultivated plants, such as cucumber (<i>C. sativus</i> L.), melon (<i>C. melo</i> L.), watermelon (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i> (Thunb.) Matsum. &amp; Nakai) and squash and pumpkin (<i>Cucurbita</i> spp.). Agricultural production of cucurbits uses 9 million hectares of land and yields 184 million tons of vegetables, fruits and seeds annually (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a>). The cucurbit family also displays a rich diversity of sex expression, and the cucumber has served as a primary model system for sex determination studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tanurdzic, M. &amp; Banks, J.A. Sex-determining mechanisms in land plants. Plant Cell 16, S61âS71 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1635">1</a></sup>. The cucurbits are also model plants for the study of vascular biology, as both xylem and phloem sap can be readily collected for studies of long-distance signaling events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lough, T.J. &amp; Lucas, W.J. Integrative plant biology: role of phloem long-distance macromolecular trafficking. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 203â232 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1639">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Xoconostle-CÃ¡zares, B. et al. Plant paralog to viral movement protein that potentiates rransport of mRNA into the phloem. Science 283, 94â98 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1642">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite the agricultural and biological importance of cucurbits, knowledge of their genetics and genome is currently very limited. We have therefore sequenced and assembled the genome of the domestic cucumber, <i>C. sativus</i> var. <i>sativus</i> L.</p><p>All previous plant genome sequences have been derived using traditional Sanger technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1658">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1661">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1664">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1667">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1670">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79â92 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1673">9</a></sup>. The recent development of next-generation sequencing technologies has significantly improved sequencing throughput at a markedly reduced cost<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shendure, J., Mitra, R.D., Varma, C. &amp; Church, G.M. Advanced sequencing technologies: methods and goals. Nat. Rev. Genet. 5, 335â344 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1677">10</a></sup>. However, an intrinsic characteristic of next-generation technologies is their short read length (<span class="stix">â¼</span>50 bp), which prevents their direct application for <i>de novo</i> assembly of large genomes. When using these new technologies, assembly is typically carried out by mapping these short reads onto a known reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bentley, D.R. et al. Accurate whole human genome sequencing using reversible terminator chemistry. Nature 456, 53â59 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1684">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wang, J. et al. The diploid genome sequence of an Asian individual. Nature 456, 60â65 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1687">12</a></sup>. For the cucumber genome, we carried out a novel combination <i>de novo</i> sequencing strategy, taking advantage of the long read and clone length of Sanger technology and, for the first time, the high sequencing depth and low unit cost of Illumina GA technology.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We selected the 'Chinese long' inbred line 9930, which is commonly used in modern cucumber breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Staub, J.E., Serquen, F.C., Horejsi, T. &amp; Chen, J.-f. Genetic diversity in cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.): IV. An evaluation of Chinese germplasm1. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 46, 297â310 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1706">13</a></sup>, for our genome sequencing project. We generated a total of 26.5 billion high-quality base pairs, or 72.2-fold genome coverage, of which the Sanger reads provided 3.9-fold coverage and the Illumina GA reads provided 68.3-fold coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The GA reads ranged in length from 42 to 53 bp.</p><p>We compared the assemblies obtained by Sanger reads only, Illumina GA reads only and Sanger plus Illumina reads. The 'hybrid' approach achieved markedly longer N50 (the size above which half of the total length of the sequence set can be found) in both contigs and scaffolds, so we used this assembly for further analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The total length of the assembled genome was 243.5 Mb, about 30% smaller than the genome size estimated by flow cytometry of isolated nuclei stained with propidium iodide (367 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1722">14</a></sup> and by <i>K</i>-mer depth distribution of sequenced reads (350 Mb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Several types of satellite sequences were present in the data set, comprising 23.2% of all Sanger reads and 76.2% of unassembled reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 3</a>). FISH analysis indicated that these are primarily located in the centromeric and telomeric regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Han, Y.H. et al. Distribution of the tandem repeat sequences and karyotyping in cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.) by fluorescence in situ hybridization. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 122, 80â88 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1736">15</a></sup>. The cucumber genome also contains a large number of rRNA sequences, and about 3.3% of the Sanger reads matched 45S rRNA. These results indicated that the majority of the remaining 30% of unassembled regions of the genome are likely to be heterochromatic satellite or rRNA sequences.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Cucumber genome assembly statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.475/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The high coverage of the cucumber genome by this assembly was also confirmed using the available EST, fosmid and BAC sequences. The assembly contains 96.8% of the 63,312 cucumber unigenes assembled from <span class="stix">â¼</span>350,000 Roche 454âsequenced ESTs, 99.3% of the 6,952 NCBI-deposited ESTs of cucumber, 91.2% of the 50,441 NCBI-deposited ESTs of melon and 98.7% of the six finished fosmid and BAC sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><p>A genetic map was developed using 77 recombinant inbred lines from the intersubspecific cross between Gy14 (a North American processing marketâtype cucumber cultivar) and PI183967 (an accession of <i>C. sativus</i> var. <i>hardwickii</i> originating from India). The map spans 581 cM and contains 1,885 markers, including 995 micro-satellite markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ren, Y. et al. An integrated genetic and cytogenetic map of the cucumber genome. PLoS One 4, e5795 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1935">16</a></sup> and 890 Diversity Arrays Technology markers (marker sequences can be accessed at <a href="http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn">http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn</a>). Using this map, we were able to anchor 72.8% of the assembled sequences onto the seven chromosomes. Among the 1,885 markers, 1,763 (93.5%) were uniquely aligned and used for constructing the pseudochromosomes. The majority (98.7%) of the markers were collinear with the sequence assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Comparison of the genetic and physical distances between markers revealed recombination suppression of two 10-Mb regions at either end of chromosome 4, a 20-Mb region on chromosome 5 and an 8-Mb region on chromosome 7. Using high-resolution FISH, we confirmed previously identified segmental inversion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ren, Y. et al. An integrated genetic and cytogenetic map of the cucumber genome. PLoS One 4, e5795 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1950">16</a></sup> within the suppression region on chromosome 5 between Gy14 and PI183967 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), which provides an explanation for recombination suppression in these regions. These regions of recombination suppression are additionally useful for studying cucumber evolution during domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Integrated genetic and physical map of cucumber."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Integrated genetic and physical map of cucumber.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="661"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Genetic versus physical distance map of the seven cucumber chromosomes. The genetic map was constructed using a recombinant inbred line mapping population from the intersubspecific cross between Gy14 (domestic cucumber) and PI183967 (wild cucumber). (<b>b</b>) Segmental inversion between Gy14 and PI183967 on cucumber chromosome 5 detected by high-resolution FISH (12-2 and 12-7 denote individual fosmid clones). A low-resolution FISH analysis was also recently reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ren, Y. et al. An integrated genetic and cytogenetic map of the cucumber genome. PLoS One 4, e5795 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17306e1975">16</a></sup>. Scale bars represent 1 Î¼m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>After excluding 16 markers whose genetic positions were ambiguous, we examined the six remaining regions that had conflicts between the genetic map and our assembly. Upon inspection, we found that clone mate-pair information supported our assembly in all of these regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). We also identified no misassembly within the regions covered by the six finished fosmid or BAC sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The conflicts may be a result of chromosomal rearrangement that occurred between the sequenced genotype 9930 and the genotypes used to create the mapping population; alternatively, these markers may have been placed incorrectly on the genetic map. Sequencing depth distribution showed that we obtained more than 10Ã coverage on more than 97.5% of the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequences and transposons</h3><p>The cucumber genome contains a large number of transposable elements, but only a few have previously been identified. We therefore constructed repeat libraries using multiple <i>de novo</i> methods and then derived a combined repeat library that contained 1,566 sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 5</a>), of which 469 (29.9%) were manually classified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 6</a>). We then used this library for repeat annotation of the cucumber genome. We identified a total of 54.4 Mb, which represents <span class="stix">â¼</span>24% of the genome, as repeats. Among them, 51.5% could be classified based on known repeats. The long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (<i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i>) made up the majority of the transposable element classes and comprised 10.4% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 7</a>). The repeats divergence rate (percentage of substitutions in the matching region compared with consensus repeats in constructed libraries) distribution showed a peak at 20%. A fraction of LTR retrotransposons, long interspersed nuclear elements and DNA transposons (composing 2.3%, 0.4% and 0.2% of the genome, respectively) are of relatively recent origin, having a sequence divergence rate of less than 5% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene annotation</h3><p>We used three gene-prediction methods (cDNA-EST, homology based and <i>ab initio</i>) to identify protein-coding genes and then built a consensus gene set by merging all of the results (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). We predicted 26,682 genes, with a mean coding sequence size of 1,046 bp and an average of 4.39 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Under an 80% sequence overlap threshold, we found that 26.7% of the genes were supported by models from all three gene prediction methods, 25% had both <i>ab initio</i> prediction and homology-based evidence, and 7.4% had <i>ab initio</i> prediction and cDNA-EST expression evidence; the remaining genes were primarily derived from pure <i>ab initio</i> prediction, but the majority of these were supported by multiple gene finders (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 9</a>). About 81% of the genes have homologs in the TrEMBL protein database, and 66% can be classified by InterPro. In sum, 82% of the genes have either known homologs or can be functionally classified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 10</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, we identified 292 rRNA fragments and 699 tRNA, 238 small nucleolar RNA, 192 small nuclear RNA and 171 miRNA genes in the cucumber genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><p>On the basis of pairwise protein sequence similarities, we carried out a gene family clustering analysis on all genes in sequenced plants, using rice as an outgroup. The cucumber genes consist of 15,669 families. Of these, 4,362 are cucumber unique families, among which 3,784 are single-gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 12</a>). The EST confirmation rate of these unique single-copy genes was much lower than the average of all predicted genes (33.4% vs. 72.3%, respectively). This category may therefore contain a number of false-positive predictions. In papaya, there are 4,622 unique families, but the actual number of genes is estimated to be 24,746, which is lower than the 28,629 predicted genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2072">7</a></sup>. Thus, the actual number in cucumber should be lower than 26,682 and similar to that in papaya. The smaller average gene family size in cucumber (1.71) and papaya (1.77) supports this conclusion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of cucumber genome with other sequenced plant genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Comparison of cucumber genome with other sequenced plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="268"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Numbers of predicted genes, numbers of tandem duplicated genes and gene family sizes of the six sequenced plant genomes. (<b>b</b>) The 4DTv distribution of duplicate gene pairs in cucumber and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, calculated based on alignment of codons with HKY substitution model.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The cucumber genome contains the smallest number of tandem gene duplications (479) among all the plants we compared, whereas grapevine has the largest number (5,382; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). This may contribute in part to the small number of genes in cucumber.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Absence of recent whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) is common in angiosperm plants and produces a tremendous source of raw material for gene genesis. Previous research has revealed a paleohexaploidy (Î³) event in the common ancestor of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and grapevine after the divergence of monocotyledons and dicotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2125">6</a></sup>. Subsequently, two WGDs (Î± and Î²) occurred in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Bowers, J.E., Chapman, B.A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2131">17</a></sup> and one (p) in poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2135">8</a></sup>, whereas no recent WGD occurred in grapevine and papaya. Evidence indicates that rice underwent an ancient WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yu, J. et al. The genomes of Oryza sativa: a history of duplications. PLoS Biol. 3, e38 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2139">18</a></sup>. We carried out a collinear gene-order analysis on the cucumber genome and observed no recent WGD and only a few segmental duplication events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). We also used the distance-transversion rate at fourfold degenerate sites (4DTv method) to analyze paralogous gene pairs between syntenic blocks in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and cucumber, respectively. Two peaks (<span class="stix">â¼</span>0.06 and <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.25) in <i>Arabidopsis</i> support the two recent WGDs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). In cucumber, the analysis showed ancient duplication events (peak at <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.60) but did not reveal recent WGD. This lack of recurrent WGD in the small cucumber genome provides an important complement to the grapevine and papaya genomes to study ancestral forms and arrangements of plant genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Synteny with flowering plant genomes</h3><p>Given the similar gene arrangements between cucumber and other plant genomes, we defined syntenic blocks that contained 5,473, 6,525, 9,842, 8,439 and 3,992 cucumber genes collinear to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, papaya, poplar, grapevine and rice, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Figs. 8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">12</a>). The numbers of collinear genes were consistent with the phylogenetic distances of the other plants to cucumber. Within the syntenic blocks, we observed the highest density of collinear genes between cucumber and grapevine (90.5 genes per Mb), followed by papaya (76.1; the low contiguity of genome assembly may have, in part, decreased this value), poplar (68.8), rice (55.6) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (43.5; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 13</a>). This indicates that <i>Arabidopsis</i> has the most reshuffled or rearranged genome, whereas the genomes of grapevine and papaya are more conserved, probably because they have not undergone WGD since the ancestral paleohexaploidy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Substantial fusion events involved in chromosomal evolution</h3><p>Melon and cucumber belong to the same genus, although cucumber has seven chromosomes and melon has 12. Watermelon, their common distant relative, has 11 chromosomes. To investigate cucurbit chromosomal evolution, we compared the melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fernandez-Silva, I. et al. Bin mapping of genomic and EST-derived SSRs in melon (Cucumis melo L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 139 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2204">19</a></sup> and watermelon genetic maps to the cucumber genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). In total, 348 (66.7%) of the 522 melon markers and 136 (58.6%) of the 232 watermelon markers were aligned on the cucumber chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The comparison revealed that there has been no substantial rearrangement of cucumber chromosome 7, which corresponds to melon chromosome 1 and watermelon group 7.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparative genomic analysis of cucurbits."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparative genomic analysis of cucurbits.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="312"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparative analysis of the melon and watermelon genetic maps with the cucumber sequence map. Cucumber, melon and watermelon have 7, 12 and 11 pairs of chromosomes, respectively. The current version of the watermelon genetic map is organized into 18 genetic groups. (<b>b</b>) Syntenic blocks between the cucumber genome and a melon BAC sequence (GenBank accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=EF188258.1">EF188258.1</a>). Genes are indicated by black arrows with the orientation indicated on the sequence. Rectangles, transposable elements; red, retrotransposable elements; blue, DNA transposons; green, unclassified transposable elements. Orthologous sequence regions between the two genomes are shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using watermelon as an outgroup, we found that cucumber chromosomes 1, 2, 3, 5 and 6 were collinear to melon chromosomes 2 and 12, 3 and 5, 4 and 6, 9 and 10, and 8 and 11, respectively, indicating that after speciation these cucumber chromosomes each resulted from a fusion of two ancestral chromosomes. We also found that cucumber chromosome 6 and melon chromosome 3 have a syntenic segment, indicating that interchromosome rearrangement occurred in one of the two genomes after speciation. Cucumber chromosome 4 largely corresponds to melon chromosome 7, although a segment of melon chromosome 8 is syntenic with cucumber chromosome 4 (crossing the centromere). These data indicate that the rearrangement is most likely to have occurred before the divergence of cucumber and melon. In addition to chromosome fusion and interchromosome rearrangements, the comparison revealed the occurrence of several intrachromosome rearrangements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Cucumber-melon microsynteny</h3><p>To estimate the sequence divergence rate, we compared the four sequenced melon BACs to the cucumber genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). There are 56 genes on the melon BACs, 52 of which are collinear with the cucumber genome. The mean sequence similarity over coding regions is 95%. Although the gene region similarity is very high, the repeat content between the two genomes is quite different. New transposable elements were frequently inserted in the intergenic regions of both genomes. Hence, only 54% of the BAC sequences could be aligned onto the cucumber genome, with an average of 88% sequence identity. Nonetheless, the highly conserved gene content and order between the two species make the cucumber genome useful for genetic analysis of melon.</p><p>Using the annotated genes in the four melon BACs, we obtained and manually curated eight orthologous families among rice, cucumber, melon, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and papaya. Extrapolating from the age of divergence between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and papaya (54â90 million years ago), we estimated that cucumber and melon diverged about 4â7 million years ago, which is consistent with a previous estimate of 9 Â± 3 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schaefer, H., Heibl, C. &amp; Renner, S.S. Gourds afloat: a dated phylogeny reveals an Asian origin of the gourd family (Cucurbitaceae) and numerous oversea dispersal events. Proc. Biol. Sci. 276, 843â851 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2276">20</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Pathogen resistance genes</h3><p>Only 61 nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-containing resistance (NBS-R) genes have been identified in cucumber, similar to papaya (55)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2288">7</a></sup> but only a fraction of what is found in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (200), poplar (398) and rice (600)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2295">8</a></sup>. Distribution of NBS genes on chromosomes is nonrandom, with only five genes located on chromosomes 1, 6 and 7 and 20 genes located on chromosome 2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Three-quarters of the NBS genes are located within 11 clusters, indicating that they evolved through tandem duplications, similar to other known plant genomes.</p><p>The lipoxygenase (LOX) pathway has an important role in developmentally and environmentally regulated processes in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Liavonchanka, A. &amp; Feussner, I. Lipoxygenases: occurrence, functions and catalysis. J. Plant Physiol. 163, 348â357 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2305">21</a></sup> and generates short-chain aldehydes and alcohols that are involved in plant defense and pest resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schwab, W., Davidovich-Rikanati, R. &amp; Lewinsohn, E. Biosynthesis of plant-derived flavor compounds. Plant J. 54, 712â732 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2309">22</a></sup>. The <i>LOX</i> gene family has been notably expanded in the cucumber genome (23 <i>LOX</i> genes in cucumber, 6 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, 15 in papaya, 21 in poplar, 18 in grapevine and 15 in rice). Fourteen of the <i>LOX</i> genes are specific to the cucumber lineage. The majority of cucumber <i>LOX</i> genes (19 of 23) are distributed in three clusters, the largest of which contains 11 members that are arranged in tandem (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). The other sequenced plant genomes show no obvious <i>LOX</i> clustering, with the exception of grapevine, which has one cluster harboring six copies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Lineage-specific expansion of the LOX gene family in the five sequenced dicot genomes and rice genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Lineage-specific expansion of the <i>LOX</i> gene family in the five sequenced dicot genomes and rice genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.475/MediaObjects/41588_2009_Article_BFng475_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="600" height="648"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The <i>LOX</i> family is divided into two groups, type I and type II. The two tandem duplicated gene clusters are ordered and shown on chromosomes 2 and 4, as well as one unmapped scaffold of the cucumber genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.475/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given that the cucumber has only 61 NBS-R genes, the expanded lipoxygenase pathway might be a complementary mechanism to cope with biotic stress. In support of this hypothesis, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has more NBS-R genes and fewer <i>LOX</i> genes than does papaya. The volatile (<i>E</i>,<i>Z</i>)-2,6-nonadienal (NDE) gives cucumber its 'fresh green' flavor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Buescher, R.H. &amp; Buescher, R.W. Production and stability of (E, Z)-2, 6-nonadienal, the major flavor volatile of cucumbers. J. Food Sci. 66, 357â361 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2377">23</a></sup> and confers resistance to some bacteria and fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cho, M.J., Buescher, R.W., Johnson, M. &amp; Janes, M. Inactivation of pathogenic bacteria by cucumber volatiles (E,Z)-2,6-nonadienal and (E)-2-nonenal. J. Food Prot. 67, 1014â1016 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2382">24</a></sup>. Lipoxygenase and one type of hydroperoxide lyase, 9-HPL, synthesize NDE from linolenic acid precursors. Genes encoding enzymes with 9-HPL activity are rarely found in other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Matsui, K. et al. Fatty acid 9- and 13-hydroperoxide lyases from cucumber. FEBS Lett. 481, 183â188 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2386">25</a></sup>. However, cucumber contains two tandem <i>HPL</i> genes, one of which has been experimentally confirmed as encoding an enzyme with 9-HPL activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Matsui, K. et al. Fatty acid 9- and 13-hydroperoxide lyases from cucumber. FEBS Lett. 481, 183â188 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2393">25</a></sup>. The expansion of the <i>LOX</i> gene family and the duplicated <i>HPL</i> genes may be related to the high level of NDE synthesis in cucumber.</p><p>Eukaryotic translation initiation factors, particularly the eIF4E and eIF4G families, confer recessive resistance to plant RNA virus infections. An <i>EIF4E</i> gene in melon was found to mediate recessive resistance against melon necrotic spot virus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Nieto, C. et al. An eIF4E allele confers resistance to an uncapped and non-polyadenylated RNA virus in melon. Plant J. 48, 452â462 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2409">26</a></sup>. In the cucumber genome, three <i>EIF4E</i> and three <i>EIF4G</i> genes have been identified, providing candidates for known recessive resistance genes against RNA viruses such as zucchini yellow mosaic virus and watermelon mosaic virus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wai, T. &amp; Grumet, R. Inheritance of resistance to watermelon mosaic virus in the cucumber line TMG-1: tissue-specific expression and relationship to zucchini yellow mosaic virus resistance. Theor. Appl. Genet. 91, 699â706 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2419">27</a></sup>. In some wild melon genotypes, enhanced expression of two glyoxylate aminotransferase genes (<i>At1</i> and <i>At2</i>) controls the resistance to downy mildew, a devastating foliar disease of cucurbits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Taler, D., Galperin, M., Benjamin, I., Cohen, Y. &amp; Kenigsbuch, D. Plant eR genes that encode photorespiratory enzymes confer resistance against disease. Plant Cell 16, 172â184 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2430">28</a></sup>. We identified two <i>At</i> homologs in cucumber that could be candidate genes for downy mildew resistance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Novel biosynthetic pathways</h3><p>Cucurbitacins are bitter cucurbit triterpenoid compounds that are toxic to most organisms but can attract specialized insects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Balkema-Boomstra, A.G. et al. Role of cucurbitacin C in resistance to spider mite Tetranychus urticae in cucumber Cucumis sativus L. J. Chem. Ecol. 29, 225â235 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2445">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Da Costa, C.P. &amp; Jones, C.M. Cucumber beetle resistance and mite susceptibility controlled by the bitter gene in Cucumis sativus L. Science 172, 1145â1146 (1971)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2448">30</a></sup>. The presence of cucurbitacin in the cucumber is controlled by a mendelian gene, <i>Bi</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Da Costa, C.P. &amp; Jones, C.M. Cucumber beetle resistance and mite susceptibility controlled by the bitter gene in Cucumis sativus L. Science 172, 1145â1146 (1971)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2454">30</a></sup>. Oxidosqualene cyclase catalyzes the formation of the triterpene carbon framework in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Phillips, D.R., Rasbery, J.M., Bartel, B. &amp; Matsuda, S.P. Biosynthetic diversity in plant triterpene cyclization. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 9, 305â314 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2458">31</a></sup>. An <i>OSC</i> gene, <i>CPQ</i>, in squash (<i>Cucurbita pepo</i> L.) is the first committed enzyme in the cucurbitacin biosynthesis pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Shibuya, M., Adachi, S. &amp; Ebizuka, Y. Cucurbitadienol synthase, the first committed enzyme for cucurbitacin biosynthesis, is a distinct enzyme from cycloartenol synthase for phytosterol biosynthesis. Tetrahedron 60, 6995â7003 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2472">32</a></sup>. In cucumber, we identified four <i>OSC</i> genes; the <i>CPQ</i> ortholog <i>Csa008595</i> resides in a genetic interval that defines the <i>Bi</i> gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Notably, <i>Csa008595</i> forms a cluster that contains an acyltransferase-encoding gene (<i>Csa008594</i>) and two cytochrome P450âencoding genes (<i>Csa008596</i> and <i>Csa008597</i>). Three of these (<i>Csa008594</i>, <i>Csa008595</i> and <i>Csa008597</i>) are coexpressed strongly in cucumber leaf tissue (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>) in a pattern similar to that of the operon-like gene cluster involved in thalianol biosynthesis in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Field, B. &amp; Osbourn, A.E. Metabolic diversificationâindependent assembly of operon-like gene clusters in different plants. Science 320, 543â547 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2519">33</a></sup>. This gene cluster may therefore catalyze the stepwise formation of cucurbitacin in cucumber.</p><p>Cucumber is a model system for studying sex expression in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tanurdzic, M. &amp; Banks, J.A. Sex-determining mechanisms in land plants. Plant Cell 16, S61âS71 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2526">1</a></sup>. Ethylene stimulates femaleness and is considered the sex hormone of cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Rudich, J., Halevy, A.H. &amp; Kedar, N. Ethylene evolution from cucumber plants as related to sex expression. Plant Physiol. 49, 998â999 (1972)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2530">34</a></sup>. We identified 137 cucumber genes that are related to the biosynthetic and signaling pathways of ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pirrung, M.C. Ethylene biosynthesis from 1-aminocyclopropanecarboxylic acid. Acc. Chem. Res. 32, 711â718 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2534">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stepanova, A.N. &amp; Alonso, J.M. Ethylene signaling pathway. Sci. STKE 2005, cm3 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2537">36</a></sup>, but we found no gene family expansion in these pathways compared with other sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Thus, the origin of monoecy in cucumber might involve other evolutionary mechanisms.</p><p>The melon gene <i>Cm-ACS7</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Boualem, A. et al. A conserved mutation in an ethylene biosynthesis enzyme leads to andromonoecy in melons. Science 321, 836â838 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2549">37</a>) and its cucumber ortholog <i>Cs-ACS2</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, Z. et al. Molecular isolation of the M gene suggests that a conserved-residue conversion induces the formation of bisexual flowers in cucumber plants. Genetics 182, 1381â1385 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2555">38</a>) encode 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate synthase (ACS), a key regulatory enzyme in the ethylene biosynthetic pathway. Both genes are crucial to the inhibition of male organs and development of the female flower. <i>In situ</i> mRNA hybridization experiments revealed that both <i>Cm-ACS7</i> and <i>Cs-ACS2</i> transcripts accumulate only in the pistil and ovule, whereas their <i>Arabidopsis</i> ortholog, <i>AT4G26200</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>), is expressed only in the roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yamagami, T. et al. Biochemical diversity among the 1-amino-cyclopropane-1-carboxylate synthase isozymes encoded by the Arabidopsis gene family. J. Biol. Chem. 278, 49102â49112 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2578">39</a></sup>. We also identified two ethylene-responsive elements (AWTTCAAA) and one flower meristem identity gene <i>LEAFY</i>-responsive element (CCAATGT) within the <i>Cs-ACS2</i> and <i>Cm-ACS7</i> promoter sequences, but these were absent from the promoter of <i>AT4G26200</i>. These findings indicate that the evolution of unisexual flowers in cucurbits may have involved the acquisition of new <i>cis</i> elements of the <i>ACS</i> genes.</p><p>To better understand the mechanism of sex determination in cucumber, we sequenced 359,105 EST sequences from near-isogenic unisexual and bisexual flower buds using the 454 pyrosequencing technology. Our analysis revealed that six auxin-related genes (auxin can regulate sex expression by stimulating ethylene production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Takahashi, H. &amp; Jaffe, M.J. Further studies of auxin and ACC induced feminization in the cucumber plant using ethylene inhibitors. Phyton (Buenos Aires) 44, 81â86 (1984)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2604">40</a></sup>) and three short-chain dehydrogenase or reductase genes (homologs to the sex determination gene <i>ts2</i> in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="DeLong, A., Calderon-Urrea, A. &amp; Dellaporta, S.L. Sex determination gene TASSELSEED2 of maize encodes a short-chain alcohol dehydrogenase required for stage-specific floral organ abortion. Cell 74, 757â768 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2611">41</a></sup>) are more highly expressed in unisexual flowers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 16</a>). This analysis provides an important resource for further study of sex determination in cucumber.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Novel developmental programs</h3><p>The tendril is a specific climbing tool of vines, such as Vitaceae and all Cucurbitaceae. Darwin considered tendrils a key innovation in plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Darwin, C.R. The Movements and Habits of Climbing Plants (Murray, London, 1875)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2626">42</a></sup>. In cucumber and grapevine, gibberellic acid regulates tendril formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Boss, P.K. &amp; Thomas, M.R. Association of dwarfism and floral induction with a grape /'green revolution/' mutation. Nature 416, 847â850 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2630">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Galun, E. The cucumber tendrilâa new test organ for gibberellic acid. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 15, 184â185 (1959)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2633">44</a></sup>. In most plants, the transition of GA<sub>12</sub>-aldehyde to GA<sub>12</sub> is catalyzed by cytochrome P450 monooxygenase. In cucurbits, it is also catalyzed by specific GA-7-oxidase genes, which are absent from <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lange, T. Cloning gibberellin dioxygenase genes from pumpkin endosperm by heterologous expression of enzyme activities in Escherichia coli. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 94, 6553â6558 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2643">45</a></sup>. Cucumber has two GA-7-oxidase genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 17</a>). GA-20-oxidase controls key steps leading to bioactive GA<sub>1</sub> and GA<sub>4</sub>, and our data show that the cucumber has three lineage-specific clades (three copies; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). These specific genes might be associated with the role of gibberellic acid in the regulation of tendril formation. Tendril coiling involves rapid cell wall modification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Braam, J. In touch: plant responses to mechanical stimuli. New Phytol. 165, 373â389 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2658">46</a></sup>, and expansins are cell wallâloosening proteins in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Cosgrove, D.J. Loosening of plant cell walls by expansins. Nature 407, 321â326 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2662">47</a></sup>. We found that, in cucumber, the expansin subfamily EXLA has undergone marked expansion through tandem duplication (eight genes in cucumber, compared with one to three genes in other genomes; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>); this event may have contributed to the development of tendril coiling in cucumber.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Use in plant vascular biology studies</h3><p>The evolution of the plant vascular system, comprising xylem and phloem tissues, had a pivotal role in the emergence of land plants. The sieve tube system of phloem, the equivalent of the animal arterial system, delivers nutrients and signaling molecules to developing organs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lough, T.J. &amp; Lucas, W.J. Integrative plant biology: role of phloem long-distance macromolecular trafficking. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 203â232 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2679">2</a></sup>. A BLASTP analysis of 1,209 protein fragments from pumpkin phloem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lin, M.-K., Lee, Y.-J., Lough, T.J., Phinney, B.S. &amp; Lucas, W.J. Analysis of the pumpkin phloem proteome provides insights into angiosperm sieve tube function. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 8, 343â356 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2683">48</a></sup> identified 800 phloem proteins in the cucumber genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM304">Supplementary Table 18</a>). Using these cucumber proteins, we conducted orthologous gene family (cluster) analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM305">Supplementary Table 19</a>) with their homologs in other vascular plants as well as the nonvascular moss <i>Physcomitrella patens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Rensing, S.A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64â69 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2695">49</a></sup>. In total, we constructed 686 clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Tab2">Table 2</a>). About two-thirds (49 of 75) of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> and half (57 of 120) of the rice phloem proteins identified in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Aki, T., Shigyo, M., Nakano, R., Yoneyama, T. &amp; Yanagisawa, S. Nano scale proteomics revealed the presence of regulatory proteins including rhree FT-Like proteins in phloem and xylem saps from rice. Plant Cell Physiol. 49, 767â790 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2706">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Giavalisco, P., Kapitza, K., Kolasa, A., Buhtz, A. &amp; Kehr, J. Towards the proteome of Brassica napus phloem sap. Proteomics 6, 896â909 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17306e2709">51</a></sup> were included in this data set, indicating the effectiveness of these analyses and the value of this resource for vascular biology studies in plants.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of orthologous gene families (clusters) established using cucumber genes homologous to pumpkin phloem proteins</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.475/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The vascular and nonvascular plants shared 596 clusters; between monocots and eudicots, there are 648 clusters in common. Phloem protein II (<i>PP2</i>; cluster 2432) are present in angiosperms but absent from the moss genome. <i>PP2</i>-like genes are also present in gymnosperm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Dinant, S. et al. Diversity of the superfamily of phloem lectins (phloem protein 2) in angiosperms. Plant Physiol. 131, 114â128 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3007">52</a></sup>, indicating their association with the advent of vascular plants. In cucurbits, these genes can increase the size-exclusion limit of plasmodesmata and facilitate cell-to-cell traffic of macromolecules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Dinant, S. et al. Diversity of the superfamily of phloem lectins (phloem protein 2) in angiosperms. Plant Physiol. 131, 114â128 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3011">52</a></sup> and thus are likely to have an essential role in vascular function. The sieve element occlusion proteins (gene cluster 4754), present in all eudicots but absent from mosses and monocots, represent a novel mechanism that evolved for sealing the sieve tube system after wounding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="PÃ©lissier, H.C., Peters, W.S., Collier, R., van Bel, A.J. &amp; Knoblauch, M. GFP tagging of sieve element occlusion (SEO) proteins results in green fluorescent forisomes. Plant Cell Physiol. 49, 1699â1710 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3015">53</a></sup>.</p><p>The average number of genes in each cluster ranges from 2.9 to 5.1 in the vascular plants, compared to 1.7 in moss (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#Tab2">Table 2</a>). The increase of gene numbers per cluster may be associated with the evolution of the plant vascular system. The 16-kDa PP16 cluster (cluster 2599) has an average of 3.7 genes in the vascular plants compared to 2 in moss. The <i>CmPP16</i> gene in pumpkin is involved in transport of mRNA into the phloem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Xoconostle-CÃ¡zares, B. et al. Plant paralog to viral movement protein that potentiates rransport of mRNA into the phloem. Science 283, 94â98 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3028">3</a></sup>. The increase of the number of <i>PP16</i> genes in vascular plants indicates these new members may be involved in long-distance trafficking of mRNA.</p><p>To better understand xylem formation, we compared gene families related to lignin and cellulose biosynthesis between woody and herbaceous plants. The perennial woody plants, poplar and grapevine, have a large number of lignin biosynthesisârelated genes (48 and 49, respectively), whereas the semiwoody plant papaya has an intermediate number (39). In contrast, the herbaceous plants <i>Arabidopsis</i> and cucumber have smaller numbers (28 and 26, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Among these gene families, the number of genes in the cadmium-sensitive <i>CAD</i> family was consistent with this trend. In poplar and grapevine, homologs for <i>AT4G37980</i> and <i>AT4G37990</i> in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, which have low cadmium-sensitive enzymatic activity <i>in vitro</i> and may have only a minor role in lignin formation in this species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Kim, S.J. et al. Expression of cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenases and their putative homologues during Arabidopsis thaliana growth and development: lessons for database annotations. Phytochemistry 68, 1957â1974 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3060">54</a></sup>, were expanded markedly. In papaya, there is an expansion of homologs for <i>AT1G37970</i>, which lack detectable cadmium-sensitive catalytic activities <i>in vitro</i> but are expressed predominantly in lignin-forming tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Kim, S.J. et al. Expression of cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenases and their putative homologues during Arabidopsis thaliana growth and development: lessons for database annotations. Phytochemistry 68, 1957â1974 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3070">54</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). Thus, the expansion of <i>CAD</i> genes may be associated with wood formation. It is also notable that grapevine has the largest <i>PAL</i> gene family, with 15 members, and that poplar and papaya have the largest number of <i>HCT</i> genes, with 7 members. Of the cellulose biosynthesisârelated genes, poplar has more <i>CESA</i> and <i>COB</i> genes (18 of each) than do any of the other sequenced dicots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.475#MOESM303">Supplementary Table 20</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>The sequence of the cucumber genome provides an invaluable new resource for biological research and breeding of cucurbits. The high collinearity between cucumber and melon genomes enables cucumber to serve as a model system in the Cucurbitaceae family for comparative genomics studies in plants. The cucumber genome and related transcriptome analysis can provide insights into the mechanisms underlying sex determination, an important biological process that has been well characterized in cucumber at the phenotypic level. The genome can also advance our knowledge of the evolution and function of the plant vascular system.</p><p>We have also shown that, in combination with traditional Sanger sequencing, next-generation DNA sequencing technologies can be used effectively for <i>de novo</i> sequencing of plant genomes, making it possible to carry out rapid and low-cost sequencing for other important plant species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Removal of contamination for Sanger reads.</h3><p>Sanger reads were aligned against mitochondrion (assembled by us based on the gene sequences of mitochondria of rice and <i>Arabidopsis</i>), chloroplast (GenBank accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AJ970307">AJ970307</a>) and satellite (GenBank <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=X03768">X03768</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=X03769">X03769</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=X03770">X03770</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=X69163">X69163</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AY424361">AY424361</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AY424362">AY424362</a>) sequences. Reads with identity &gt;95% were filtered.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17"><i>De novo</i> assembly of Solexa data.</h3><p>The De Bruijn graph method was used to represent all possible sequences assembled by Solexa reads, with a <i>K</i>-mer as a node and the (<i>K</i> â 1) base overlap between two <i>K</i>-mers as an edge. Some tips and low-coverage <i>K</i>-mers in the graph were removed to reduce sequencing errors and eliminate branches. The De Bruijn graph was then converted to a contiging graph by turning a series of linearly connected <i>K</i>-mers into a precontig node. Dijkstra's algorithm was implemented to detect bubbles, which were then straightforwardly merged into a single path if sequences of the branches were sufficiently similar. By this approach, the repeat regions could be assembled into consensus sequences.</p><p>Contigs were next connected by paired reads to form a scaffolding graph. Edges in this graph were connections between contigs, and the edge length was estimated from the insert size of the paired reads. The paired-end information was used step by step, from insert sizes around 200 bp and 500 bp to 2 kb. At each step, two procedures were applied: the repeat-masking method masked the complicated connections around repeat contigs, and the subgraph linearization turned the interleaving contigs into linear structure. This process yielded the final set of Solexa contigs and scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Combination of Sanger reads and Solexa scaffolds.</h3><p>RePS2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang, J. et al. RePS: a sequence assembler that masks exact repeats identified from the shotgun data. Genome Res. 12, 824â831 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.475#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17306e3213">55</a>) software was used to assemble the Solexa scaffolds and Sanger reads. We counted the depth of each 17-mer in the 3.9Ã plasmid and fosmid ends to create the 17-mer database, which contained all the depth information of the 17-mers. This database was then used to check all the contigs to identify repeated ones. A contig was defined as a repeat if over 80% of the 17-mers it contained were with higher depth than the threshold. After removing the repeat contigs, the scaffolds were divided into fake paired reads with read length of 600 bp and insert size of 1,700 bp. All segments over 200 bp were put into the second data set, which was then assembled as a unique region. In the same way as the construction of Solexa scaffolds, the plasmid, fosmid and BAC ends were used, step by step, to construct a 'superscaffold'.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Misassembly checking and gap filling.</h3><p>In the final stage, we used the repeat sequences to fill the gaps in the scaffolds using the following steps. First, we mapped all of the reads that contained paired-end information (Solexa and plasmid reads, as well as fosmid and BAC ends) to the scaffolds, and we used the unique contigs to establish the paired-end relationship between the contigs.
